AI: 这个视频内容非常长（1小时10分），我已经获取了完整的字幕。以下是总结：

---

## 📺 AI Builder Demo Day 全程回放 — 总结

**发布者：** 飞书（B站） | **时长：** 1:10:51 | **播放量：** 672

这是飞书社区举办的第二期 **AI Builder Demo Day**，主题是"工作都被进化成啥样了"，展示了 AI Agent 结合飞书 CLI 的各种真实落地场景。

### 🎤 嘉宾分享概览

**1. 张咋啦（ZARA）— 飞书画板 + Codex 接入飞书**
- 制作了 **beautiful-feishu-whiteboard** skill，包含 30+ 种视觉风格（克制/平衡/大胆三类）
- 用飞书画板替代 PPT、海报、论文讲解、代码架构图、会议纪要等
- 演示了如何将 Codex 和 Claude Code 接入飞书，可在聊天/文档/群聊中直接调用

**2. 张玲（Keep HR）— 零代码搭建福利广场**
- 用飞书妙搭 + 智能伙伴 + 工作流，1 个月（实际约 2 周）搭建员工福利平台
- 免密登录、自动带出个人信息、隐私加密保护
- 踩坑经验：先定设计风格再做功能、用 API 关联 HR 系统自动更新

**3. 赵迪 Katrina（可梦AI）— 自媒体视频剪辑自动化**
- 用 Codex + 飞书 CLI 自动将飞书妙记视频剪成高光集锦
- 输出到剪映草稿，可直接编辑发布
- 推荐工具：闪电说（语音输入）、Ghosti

**4. 电波曲奇 Eric — 飞书画板做代码架构可视化**
- 用长上下文模型分析代码库，自动生成飞书画板架构图
- 演示了 Claude Code、DeepFlow 等开源项目的全景架构图
- 强调"一图抵千言"，可对内协作也可对外展示

**5. Joyce（孙悦旦）— 内容运营智能体**
- 用 Codex + 飞书 CLI 搭建手机端可用的内容创作工作流
- 链路：热点发现 → AI 二创 → 生成图文/口播视频 → 沉淀到飞书多维表格
- 飞书是入口，Skill 是执行层，多维表格是沉淀层

**6. 卡昂 — AI 驱动的个人知识系统"AI通讯社"**
- 自动记录与 AI 的对话观点到飞书多维表格
- 每周自动从碎片观点中生成一篇有洞见的深度文章
- 实现"输入 → 沉淀 → 输出"的知识飞轮

**7. 飞书官方产品经理 黄觉 — Markdown 文档支持**
- 飞书云文档新增 **Markdown 类型**，支持本地 MD 文件上传/同步
- 可双向转换：MD ↔ 云文档，支持团队协作和编辑
- 支持 AI agent 直接修改 MD 内容并同步回本地

### 💡 核心趋势
- **飞书 CLI + Coding Agent（Claude Code/Codex）** 成为 AI 工作流标配
- **飞书画板** 正在替代传统 PPT 和流程图工具
- 非技术背景（如 HR）也能用 AI 搭建企业级应用
- 所有 skill 和文档都已发布到社区群中共享

AI: [{'name': 'read_file', 'args': {'file_path': '/Users/chunming.liu/learn-openspec/OpenSpec/openspec/explorations/workspace-architecture.md', 'limit': 200}, 'id': 'call_84ebfa6b72184fd3a11a1c60', 'type': 'tool_call'}, {'name': 'read_file', 'args': {'file_path': '/Users/chunming.liu/learn-openspec/OpenSpec/openspec/config.yaml'}, 'id': 'call_ad464339f2bf4c468c47ce49', 'type': 'tool_call'}]
Tool:      1	# Workspace Exploration
     2	
     3	## Context
     4	
     5	While simplifying skill installation, we identified deeper questions about how profiles, config, and workspaces should work together. This doc captures what we've decided, what's open, and what needs research.
     6	
     7	**Update:** Initial exploration revealed that "workspaces" isn't primarily about config layering—it's about a more fundamental question: **where do specs and changes live when work spans multiple modules or repositories?**
     8	
     9	---
    10	
    11	## Part 1: Profile & Config (Original Scope)
    12	
    13	### What We've Decided
    14	
    15	#### Profile UX (Simplified)
    16	
    17	**Before (original proposal):**
    18	```
    19	openspec profile set core|extended
    20	openspec profile install <workflow>
    21	openspec profile uninstall <workflow>
    22	openspec profile list
    23	openspec profile show
    24	openspec config set delivery skills|commands|both
    25	openspec config get delivery
    26	openspec config list
    27	```
    28	8 subcommands, two concepts (profile + config)
    29	
    30	**After (simplified):**
    31	```
    32	openspec config profile          # interactive picker (delivery + workflows)
    33	openspec config profile core     # preset shortcut
    34	openspec config profile extended # preset shortcut
    35	```
    36	1 command with presets, one concept
    37	
    38	#### Interactive Picker
    39	
    40	```
    41	$ openspec config profile
    42	
    43	Delivery: [skills] [commands] [both]
    44	                              ^^^^^^
    45	
    46	Workflows: (space to toggle, enter to save)
    47	[x] propose
    48	[x] explore
    49	[x] apply
    50	[x] archive
    51	[ ] new
    52	[ ] ff
    53	[ ] continue
    54	[ ] verify
    55	[ ] sync
    56	[ ] bulk-archive
    57	[ ] onboard
    58	```
    59	
    60	One place to configure both delivery method and workflow selection.
    61	
    62	#### Why "Profile" (Not "Workflows")
    63	
    64	Profiles as an abstraction allow for future extensibility:
    65	- Methodology bundles (spec-driven, test-driven)
    66	- User-created profiles
    67	- Shareable profiles
    68	- Different skill/command sets for different approaches
    69	
    70	### Config Layering Research
    71	
    72	We researched how similar tools handle config layering:
    73	
    74	| Tool | Model | Key Pattern |
    75	|------|-------|-------------|
    76	| **VSCode** | User → Workspace → Folder | Objects merge, primitives override. Workspace = committed `.vscode/` in repo |
    77	| **ESLint (flat)** | Single root config | *Deliberately killed cascading* - "complexity exploded exponentially" |
    78	| **Turborepo** | Root + package extends | Per-package `turbo.json` with `extends: ["//"]` for overrides |
    79	| **Nx** | Integrated vs Package-based | Two modes - shared root OR per-package. Hard to migrate from integrated. |
    80	| **pnpm** | Workspace file defines package scope | `pnpm-workspace.yaml` at the package-set root. Dependencies can be shared or per-package |
    81	| **Claude Code** | Global + Project | `~/.claude/` for global, `.claude/` per-project. No workspace tracking. |
    82	| **Kiro** | Distributed per-root | Each folder has `.kiro/`. Aggregated display, no inheritance. |
    83	
    84	**Key insight from ESLint:** The ESLint team explicitly removed cascading in flat config because cascading was a complexity nightmare. Their new model: one config at root, use glob patterns to target subdirectories.
    85	
    86	**Recommendation for profiles/config:** Two layers is enough.
    87	- **Global** = user's defaults (`~/.config/openspec/`)
    88	- **Project** = repo-level config (`.openspec/` or committed to repo)
    89	
    90	No "workspace" layer needed for config. This matches Claude Code's model.
    91	
    92	### Config Decision (For This Change)
    93	
    94	Keep it simple:
    95	1. Global profile as default for `openspec init`
    96	2. `openspec init` applies current profile to project
    97	3. No workspace tracking (yet)
    98	4. No auto-sync of existing projects
    99	
   100	This is explicit and doesn't prevent future features.
   101	
   102	---
   103	
   104	## Part 2: The Deeper Problem (Spec & Change Organization)
   105	
   106	### The Real Question
   107	
   108	The workspace question isn't about config—it's about **where specs and changes live** when:
   109	
   110	1. **Monorepos**: A spec or change might span multiple packages/apps
   111	2. **Multi-repo**: A change might span multiple repositories entirely
   112	3. **Cross-functional work**: A feature affects multiple teams (backend, web, iOS, Android)
   113	
   114	### Current OpenSpec Architecture
   115	
   116	OpenSpec currently assumes:
   117	- One `openspec/` per repo, always at root
   118	- CLI doesn't walk up directories—expects you're at root
   119	- Changes can touch ANY spec (no scoping)
   120	- Single config applies to everything
   121	- No notion of "scope" or "boundary" within a project
   122	
   123	```
   124	openspec/
   125	├── specs/
   126	│   ├── auth/spec.md           # Domain-organized specs
   127	│   ├── payments/spec.md
   128	│   └── checkout/spec.md
   129	├── changes/
   130	│   └── add-oauth/
   131	│       ├── proposal.md
   132	│       ├── design.md
   133	│       ├── tasks.md
   134	│       └── specs/             # Delta specs (can touch multiple)
   135	│           ├── auth/spec.md
   136	│           └── checkout/spec.md
   137	└── config.yaml
   138	```
   139	
   140	**This works well for single-project repos.** But what about:
   141	- Large monorepos with 50+ packages?
   142	- Multi-repo microservices?
   143	- Cross-functional features spanning multiple teams?
   144	
   145	### The Checkout/Payment Example
   146	
   147	Imagine a payment system with:
   148	- **Backend billing team**: Owns payment processing
   149	- **Web team**: Owns web checkout UX
   150	- **iOS team**: Owns iOS checkout UX
   151	- **Android team**: Owns Android checkout UX
   152	- **Cross-cutting**: The payment *contract* all clients must follow
   153	
   154	**Questions:**
   155	- Where does the shared payment contract spec live?
   156	- Where do platform-specific checkout specs live?
   157	- If iOS spec "extends" the shared contract, how is that expressed?
   158	- When the contract changes, how do downstream specs get updated?
   159	- Who owns what?
   160	
   161	### The Core Tension
   162	
   163	```
   164	                    SCOPE
   165	                      │
   166	         Narrow       │       Broad
   167	    (team/module)     │    (cross-cutting)
   168	                      │
   169	    ┌─────────────────┼─────────────────┐
   170	    │                 │                 │
   171	    │  "Our team's    │   "Shared       │
   172	    │   checkout      │    checkout     │
   173	    │   behavior"     │    contract"    │
   174	    │                 │                 │
   175	────┼─────────────────┼─────────────────┼──── OWNERSHIP
   176	    │                 │                 │
   177	    │  Easy:          │   Hard:         │
   178	    │  One team,      │   Multiple      │
   179	    │  one spec       │   stakeholders  │
   180	    │                 │                 │
   181	    └─────────────────┴─────────────────┘
   182	```
   183	
   184	---
   185	
   186	## Part 3: How Other Domains Solve This
   187	
   188	### Patterns from Research
   189	
   190	| Domain | Shared Stuff | Specific Stuff | How They Connect |
   191	|--------|-------------|----------------|------------------|
   192	| **Protobuf** | `common/` at root | `domain/service/` per service | Imports from common |
   193	| **Design Systems** | Design tokens, component names, APIs | Platform implementations | "Same properties, different rendering" |
   194	| **DDD** | Shared Kernel | Bounded Contexts | Context mapping defines relationships |
   195	| **RFCs** | Cross-cutting RFCs | Team-scoped RFCs | Different review processes |
   196	| **OpenAPI** | Base schemas | Per-service specs | `$ref` to shared definitions |
   197	
   198	### Protobuf Monorepo Pattern
   199	
   200	```
Tool:      1	schema: spec-driven
     2	
     3	context: |
     4	  Tech stack: TypeScript, Node.js (≥20.19.0), ESM modules
     5	  Package manager: pnpm
     6	  CLI framework: Commander.js
     7	
     8	  Product language:
     9	  - Write OpenSpec proposals and specs in user-facing product behavior language
    10	  - Requirements should describe the experience, observable behavior, and product contract
    11	  - Avoid implementation-negative SHALL statements when a positive user outcome can express the same rule
    12	  - Put internal mechanisms in design.md or tasks.md unless the mechanism is itself part of the user-facing contract
    13	
    14	  Cross-platform requirements:
    15	  - This tool runs on macOS, Linux, AND Windows
    16	  - Always use path.join() or path.resolve() for file paths - never hardcode slashes
    17	  - Never assume forward-slash path separators
    18	  - Tests must use path.join() for expected path values, not hardcoded strings
    19	  - Consider case sensitivity differences in file systems
    20	
    21	rules:
    22	  specs:
    23	    - Include scenarios for Windows path handling when dealing with file paths
    24	    - Requirements involving paths must specify cross-platform behavior
    25	    - Prefer user-facing product behavior and observable outcomes over internal implementation mechanics
    26	    - Include HOW details only when the mechanism is part of the product contract
    27	    - If we generate artifacts, specify deletion/modification by explicit list lookup, not pattern matching
    28	  tasks:
    29	    - Add Windows CI verification as a task when changes involve file paths
    30	    - Include cross-platform testing considerations
    31	  design:
    32	    - Document any platform-specific behavior or limitations
    33	    - Prefer Node.js path module over string manipulation for paths
    34	    - Use existing constants and lists - don't invent detection mechanisms
    35	    - Prefer explicit lookups over pattern matching or regex
    36	    - If we generate it, we track it by name in a constant
AI: [{'name': 'read_file', 'args': {'file_path': '/Users/chunming.liu/learn-openspec/OpenSpec/openspec/explorations/workspace-architecture.md', 'offset': 200, 'limit': 200}, 'id': 'call_c970e113b3f745fe9aff2b0b', 'type': 'tool_call'}]
Tool:    201	proto/
   202	├── common/              # Shared, low-churn types
   203	│   └── money.proto
   204	│   └── address.proto
   205	├── billing/             # Domain-specific
   206	│   └── service.proto
   207	└── checkout/
   208	    └── service.proto    # Imports from common/
   209	```
   210	
   211	**Key insight:** "Most engineering organizations should keep their proto files in one repo. The mental overhead stays constant instead of scaling with organization size."
   212	
   213	### Design Systems Pattern (Booking.com, Uber)
   214	
   215	> "Components can look quite different between iOS and Android, as they use native app design standards, but still share the **same exact properties in code**. This is what makes properties so powerful—it's the **one source of truth** for every component."
   216	
   217	**Key insight:** Shared spec defines the *contract* (properties, behavior). Platform specs define *implementation details* (how it looks/works on that platform).
   218	
   219	### DDD Bounded Contexts
   220	
   221	> "One context, one team. Clear ownership avoids miscommunication."
   222	
   223	**Key insight:** Specs should have clear ownership. Cross-cutting concerns use a "Shared Kernel" pattern—explicitly shared code/specs that require coordination to change.
   224	
   225	---
   226	
   227	## Part 4: Three Models for OpenSpec
   228	
   229	### Model A: Flat Root (Current)
   230	
   231	```
   232	openspec/
   233	├── specs/
   234	│   ├── checkout-contract/    # Shared contract
   235	│   ├── checkout-web/         # Web-specific
   236	│   ├── checkout-ios/         # iOS-specific
   237	│   ├── checkout-android/     # Android-specific
   238	│   ├── billing/              # Backend
   239	│   └── ... (50+ specs at root level)
   240	└── changes/
   241	```
   242	
   243	**Pros:**
   244	- Simple mental model
   245	- All specs in one place
   246	- No nesting complexity
   247	
   248	**Cons:**
   249	- Gets unwieldy at scale (50+ directories)
   250	- No clear ownership signals
   251	- Hard to see which specs are related
   252	- Naming conventions become critical (`checkout-*`)
   253	
   254	### Model B: Nested Specs (Domain → Platform)
   255	
   256	```
   257	openspec/
   258	├── specs/
   259	│   ├── checkout/
   260	│   │   ├── spec.md              # Shared contract (the "interface")
   261	│   │   ├── web/spec.md          # Web implementation spec
   262	│   │   ├── ios/spec.md          # iOS implementation spec
   263	│   │   └── android/spec.md      # Android implementation spec
   264	│   └── billing/
   265	│       └── spec.md
   266	└── changes/
   267	```
   268	
   269	**Pros:**
   270	- Clear hierarchy (shared at top, specific nested)
   271	- Related specs are co-located
   272	- Scales better visually
   273	- Ownership can follow structure
   274	
   275	**Cons:**
   276	- More complex spec references (`checkout/web` vs `checkout`)
   277	- Need to define inheritance/extension semantics
   278	- Does iOS spec "extend" base spec, or just reference it?
   279	
   280	**Open question:** What does "extends" mean?
   281	```yaml
   282	# checkout/ios/spec.md
   283	extends: ../spec.md   # Inherits all requirements?
   284	requirements:
   285	  - System SHALL support Apple Pay  # Adds to base?
   286	```
   287	
   288	### Model C: Distributed Specs (Near the Code)
   289	
   290	```
   291	monorepo/
   292	├── services/
   293	│   └── billing/
   294	│       └── openspec/specs/billing/spec.md
   295	├── clients/
   296	│   ├── web/
   297	│   │   └── openspec/specs/checkout/spec.md
   298	│   ├── ios/
   299	│   │   └── openspec/specs/checkout/spec.md
   300	│   └── android/
   301	│       └── openspec/specs/checkout/spec.md
   302	└── openspec/           # Root-level for cross-cutting
   303	    ├── specs/
   304	    │   └── checkout-contract/spec.md   # Shared contract
   305	    └── changes/        # Where do cross-cutting changes live?
   306	```
   307	
   308	**Pros:**
   309	- Specs live near the code they describe
   310	- Teams own their specs naturally
   311	- Works for multi-repo too (each repo has its own `openspec/`)
   312	
   313	**Cons:**
   314	- Cross-cutting specs are awkward (where do they go?)
   315	- Changes that span multiple `openspec/` directories = ???
   316	- Need a "workspace" concept to aggregate
   317	- Multiple `openspec/` roots to manage
   318	
   319	### Model D: Hybrid (Model B Inside Each Project + Model C Across Projects)
   320	
   321	Use one `openspec/` root per project, but allow nested specs within that root for clear ownership and shared contracts.
   322	For multi-repo work, use a workspace manifest to coordinate multiple projects without duplicating canonical specs.
   323	
   324	**Monorepo shape (single project, nested specs):**
   325	```
   326	repo/
   327	└── openspec/
   328	    ├── specs/
   329	    │   ├── contracts/
   330	    │   │   └── checkout/spec.md
   331	    │   ├── billing/
   332	    │   │   └── spec.md
   333	    │   └── checkout/
   334	    │       ├── web/spec.md
   335	    │       ├── ios/spec.md
   336	    │       └── android/spec.md
   337	    └── changes/
   338	        └── add-3ds/
   339	            ├── proposal.md
   340	            ├── design.md
   341	            ├── tasks.md
   342	            └── specs/
   343	                ├── contracts/checkout/spec.md
   344	                ├── billing/spec.md
   345	                ├── checkout/web/spec.md
   346	                ├── checkout/ios/spec.md
   347	                └── checkout/android/spec.md
   348	```
   349	
   350	**Multi-repo shape (multiple projects + workspace orchestration):**
   351	```
   352	~/work/
   353	├── contracts/
   354	│   └── openspec/
   355	│       ├── specs/checkout/spec.md
   356	│       └── changes/add-3ds-contract/
   357	├── billing-service/
   358	│   └── openspec/
   359	│       ├── specs/billing/spec.md
   360	│       └── changes/add-3ds-billing/
   361	├── web-client/
   362	│   └── openspec/
   363	│       ├── specs/checkout/spec.md
   364	│       └── changes/add-3ds-web/
   365	├── ios-client/
   366	│   └── openspec/
   367	│       ├── specs/checkout/spec.md
   368	│       └── changes/add-3ds-ios/
   369	└── payments-workspace/
   370	    └── .openspec-workspace/
   371	        ├── workspace.yaml
   372	        └── initiatives/add-3ds/links.yaml
   373	```
   374	
   375	`workspace.yaml` lists projects/roots. `links.yaml` maps one cross-cutting initiative to per-project changes.
   376	Canonical specs stay in owning repos; workspace data is coordination metadata only.
   377	
   378	**Pros:**
   379	- Clear ownership boundaries (one project owns its specs and changes)
   380	- Shared contracts can have a dedicated owner repo (no duplication as source of truth)
   381	- Works for monorepo and multi-repo with one mental model
   382	- Avoids inheritance complexity (relationships can start as explicit references)
   383	- Incremental migration path from current model
   384	
   385	**Cons:**
   386	- Requires new workspace UX for cross-repo coordination
   387	- Cross-repo feature work creates multiple change IDs to manage
   388	- Needs conventions for contracts ownership and initiative linking
   389	- Some users may expect one global "mega change" instead of linked per-project changes
   390	- Tooling must support nested spec paths in both main specs and change deltas
   391	
   392	---
   393	
   394	## Part 5: Multi-Repo Considerations
   395	
   396	For multi-repo setups, Model C (or the coordination half of Model D) is almost forced:
   397	
   398	```
   399	~/work/
   400	├── billing-service/
AI: [{'name': 'read_file', 'args': {'file_path': '/Users/chunming.liu/learn-openspec/OpenSpec/openspec/explorations/workspace-architecture.md', 'limit': 200, 'offset': 400}, 'id': 'call_de28890e63d14e75b4d11055', 'type': 'tool_call'}]
Tool:    401	│   └── openspec/specs/billing/
   402	├── web-client/
   403	│   └── openspec/specs/checkout/
   404	├── ios-client/
   405	│   └── openspec/specs/checkout/
   406	└── contracts/                    # Dedicated repo for shared specs?
   407	    └── openspec/specs/
   408	        └── checkout-contract/
   409	```
   410	
   411	### Questions for Multi-Repo
   412	
   413	1. **Where do shared specs live?**
   414	   - Dedicated "contracts" repo?
   415	   - Duplicated in each repo (drift risk)?
   416	   - One repo is "source of truth" and others reference it?
   417	
   418	2. **Where do cross-repo changes live?**
   419	   - In one of the repos? (feels wrong—biased ownership)
   420	   - In a separate "workspace" repo?
   421	   - In `~/.config/openspec/workspaces/my-platform/changes/`?
   422	
   423	3. **How do changes propagate?**
   424	   - Change to `checkout-contract` affects all client repos
   425	   - Do we need explicit dependency tracking?
   426	   - Or is this "out of band" (teams coordinate manually)?
   427	
   428	### What "Workspace" Might Mean for Multi-Repo
   429	
   430	If we add workspace support, it could be:
   431	
   432	> **A workspace is a collection of OpenSpec roots that can be operated on together.**
   433	
   434	```yaml
   435	# ~/.config/openspec/workspaces.yaml (or similar)
   436	workspaces:
   437	  my-platform:
   438	    roots:
   439	      - ~/work/billing-service
   440	      - ~/work/web-client
   441	      - ~/work/ios-client
   442	      - ~/work/contracts
   443	    shared_context: |
   444	      All services use TypeScript.
   445	      API contracts follow OpenAPI 3.1.
   446	```
   447	
   448	This would enable:
   449	1. **Cross-repo changes**: Create a change that tracks deltas across multiple roots
   450	2. **Aggregated spec view**: See all specs across workspace
   451	3. **Shared context**: Context/rules that apply to all roots
   452	
   453	---
   454	
   455	## Part 6: Key Design Questions
   456	
   457	### 1. Should specs be hierarchical (with inheritance)?
   458	
   459	**Option A: No inheritance, just organization**
   460	- Nested directories are purely organizational
   461	- Each spec is independent
   462	- Relationships are implicit (naming) or documented manually
   463	
   464	**Option B: Explicit inheritance**
   465	```yaml
   466	# checkout/ios/spec.md
   467	extends: ../spec.md
   468	requirements:
   469	  - System SHALL support Apple Pay  # Adds to base
   470	```
   471	- Child specs inherit parent requirements
   472	- Can add, override, or extend
   473	- More powerful but more complex
   474	
   475	**Option C: References without inheritance**
   476	```yaml
   477	# checkout/ios/spec.md
   478	references:
   479	  - ../spec.md  # "See also" but no inheritance
   480	requirements:
   481	  - System SHALL implement checkout per checkout-contract
   482	  - System SHALL support Apple Pay
   483	```
   484	- Explicit references for documentation
   485	- No automatic inheritance
   486	- Simpler semantics
   487	
   488	### 2. Where does the "shared kernel" live?
   489	
   490	**Option A: Root level (Model B)**
   491	- `openspec/specs/checkout/spec.md` is the shared kernel
   492	- Platform specs nest under it
   493	
   494	**Option B: Dedicated area**
   495	- `openspec/specs/_shared/checkout-contract/spec.md`
   496	- Or `openspec/specs/_contracts/checkout/spec.md`
   497	- Explicit "shared" namespace
   498	
   499	**Option C: Separate repo (Model C for multi-repo)**
   500	- A dedicated `contracts` or `specs` repo
   501	- Other repos reference it
   502	
   503	### 3. What's a "workspace" vs a "project"?
   504	
   505	If we introduce workspaces:
   506	
   507	| Concept | Definition |
   508	|---------|------------|
   509	| **Project** | Single OpenSpec root (one `openspec/` directory) |
   510	| **Workspace** | Collection of projects that can be operated on together |
   511	
   512	A workspace would enable:
   513	- Aggregated spec viewing across projects
   514	- Cross-project changes
   515	- Shared context across projects
   516	
   517	**Question:** Do we need explicit workspace tracking, or just ad-hoc multi-root (like Claude Code's `/add-dir`)?
   518	
   519	### 4. Does OpenSpec need to understand dependencies?
   520	
   521	If `checkout-web` depends on `checkout-contract`:
   522	- Should OpenSpec know this relationship?
   523	- Should a change to `checkout-contract` warn about downstream specs?
   524	- Or is dependency tracking "out of scope"?
   525	
   526	**Trade-off:**
   527	- With dependency tracking: More powerful, automatic propagation warnings
   528	- Without: Simpler, teams manage dependencies themselves
   529	
   530	### 5. How should changes work for cross-cutting work?
   531	
   532	**For monorepos (Model B):**
   533	- One change, multiple delta specs in `specs/`
   534	- Already works today
   535	
   536	**For multi-repo (Model C):**
   537	- Option A: One "workspace change" that references multiple repo changes
   538	- Option B: Separate changes in each repo that reference each other
   539	- Option C: Changes always live in one repo, reference specs in others
   540	
   541	---
   542	
   543	## Part 7: What Would "Amazing" Look Like?
   544	
   545	Based on research, teams love:
   546	
   547	1. **One place to look** (Protobuf: "mental overhead stays constant")
   548	2. **Clear ownership** (DDD: "one context, one team")
   549	3. **Shared contracts with local extensions** (Design Systems: "same properties, different rendering")
   550	4. **Automatic consistency** (Design Systems: "design tokens as foundation")
   551	5. **Low cognitive load** (shouldn't have to think about organization too much)
   552	
   553	### Possible North Stars
   554	
   555	**Ambitious:**
   556	> OpenSpec automatically understands your repo structure, detects cross-cutting specs, and helps you create changes that flow to the right places.
   557	
   558	**Simpler:**
   559	> You organize specs however you want. OpenSpec just works.
   560	
   561	**Practical:**
   562	> Nested specs for organization. Explicit dependencies for cross-cutting. No magic.
   563	
   564	---
   565	
   566	## Part 8: Possible Paths Forward
   567	
   568	### For This Change (simplify-skill-installation)
   569	
   570	Don't solve spec organization now. Keep scope to:
   571	1. Profile UX simplification
   572	2. `openspec init` improvements
   573	3. No workspace tracking yet
   574	
   575	### Future: Spec Organization Change
   576	
   577	A separate change to explore and implement:
   578	
   579	1. **Decide on Model A, B, C, or D (hybrid)**
   580	2. **Decide on inheritance semantics** (or none)
   581	3. **Update spec resolution** to handle nesting
   582	4. **Update change deltas** to handle nested specs
   583	
   584	### Future: Multi-Repo / Workspace Change
   585	
   586	If needed, a separate change for:
   587	
   588	1. **Define workspace concept**
   589	2. **Implement workspace tracking** (or ad-hoc multi-root)
   590	3. **Cross-repo changes**
   591	4. **Shared context across repos**
   592	
   593	---
   594	
   595	## Part 9: Spec Philosophy (Behavior First, Lightweight, Agent-Aligned)
   596	
   597	### What is a spec in OpenSpec?
   598	
   599	For OpenSpec, a spec should be treated as a **verifiable behavior contract at a boundary**:
   600	- What users, integrators, or operators can observe and rely on
AI: [{'name': 'read_file', 'args': {'file_path': '/Users/chunming.liu/learn-openspec/OpenSpec/openspec/explorations/workspace-architecture.md', 'limit': 200, 'offset': 600}, 'id': 'call_8883e9e5a67f41abafef016c', 'type': 'tool_call'}]
Tool:    601	- What can be validated with tests, checks, or explicit review
   602	- What should remain stable even if internal implementation changes
   603	
   604	### What should and should not be in specs
   605	
   606	**Include:**
   607	- Observable behavior and outcomes
   608	- Interface/data contracts (inputs, outputs, error conditions)
   609	- Non-functional constraints that matter externally (privacy, security, reliability)
   610	- Compatibility guarantees that downstream consumers depend on
   611	
   612	**Avoid:**
   613	- Internal implementation details (class names, library choices, control flow)
   614	- Tooling mechanics that can change without affecting behavior
   615	- Step-by-step execution plans (belongs in tasks/design)
   616	
   617	### Keep rigor proportional (to avoid bureaucracy)
   618	
   619	Use progressive rigor:
   620	
   621	1. **Lite spec (default for most changes)**
   622	   - Short behavior bullets, clear scope, and acceptance checks
   623	2. **Full spec (only for high-risk or cross-boundary work)**
   624	   - Deeper contract detail for API breaks, migrations, security/privacy, or cross-team/repo changes
   625	
   626	This keeps day-to-day usage lightweight while preserving clarity where failures are expensive.
   627	
   628	### Human exploration -> agent-authored specs
   629	
   630	OpenSpec is often agent-authored from human exploration. To make that reliable:
   631	
   632	- Humans provide intent, constraints, and examples from exploration
   633	- Agents convert that into concise, behavior-first requirements and scenarios
   634	- Agents keep implementation detail in design/tasks, not specs
   635	- Validation checks enforce structure and testability
   636	
   637	In short: humans shape intent; agents produce consistent, verifiable contracts.
   638	
   639	### Where this philosophy should live
   640	
   641	To avoid losing this in exploration notes, codify it in:
   642	1. `docs/concepts.md` for human-facing framing
   643	2. `openspec/specs/openspec-conventions/spec.md` for normative spec conventions
   644	3. `openspec/specs/docs-agent-instructions/spec.md` for agent-instruction authoring rules
   645	
   646	---
   647	
   648	## Part 10: Design Decisions (April 2026)
   649	
   650	After evaluating the models above against real multi-repo use cases (see [#725](https://github.com/Fission-AI/OpenSpec/issues/725)), we converged on the following design direction.
   651	
   652	### Core Insight
   653	
   654	The workspace itself is not the durable thing. For large teams, the durable planning object is the **initiative** or **plan**, while repo-local specs and changes remain the execution artifacts owned by each repo. The set of repos involved in a feature is typically feature-scoped and changes over time, so a static workspace manifest that must be configured before work begins creates ceremony that doesn't match how teams actually work.
   655	
   656	### Decision: Model D with Lazy Workspace
   657	
   658	Choose Model D (Hybrid) from Part 4, but make the workspace manifest **optional and lazy, not prerequisite**.
   659	
   660	- **Each repo keeps its own canonical `openspec/`** — no change to the fundamental storage model.
   661	- **Cross-root work can be coordinated through an initiative in a coordination workspace** — this is where shared planning lives when the work stops being cleanly repo-scoped.
   662	- **"Workspace" is a derived or explicit coordination view** over linked repos and linked changes, not something users must register up front.
   663	- **Persist a workspace manifest only when someone explicitly wants a reusable cross-repo bundle** — this is an opt-in convenience, not a requirement.
   664	
   665	### Decision: Initiative-First Planning with Linked Repo-Local Changes
   666	
   667	For larger multi-team work, repo-centric planning is the wrong primary abstraction. Teams and repos are many-to-many facets over the same work. OpenSpec should treat the **initiative / plan** as the first-class planning object, then link repo-local changes to it.
   668	
   669	This is especially important because a change today bundles:
   670	
   671	- `proposal.md`
   672	- `design.md`
   673	- `tasks.md`
   674	- delta specs
   675	- `.openspec.yaml`
   676	
   677	That bundled shape works well for repo-local work, but becomes awkward when one piece of work spans multiple repos or teams. In those cases, a single repo-local change is trying to act as both:
   678	
   679	- the shared planning object
   680	- the repo-specific execution artifact
   681	
   682	Those should be split.
   683	
   684	The preferred model is:
   685	
   686	```text
   687	coordination workspace /
   688	  .openspec-workspace/
   689	    workspace.yaml
   690	    initiatives/
   691	      add-3ds/
   692	        initiative.yaml
   693	        proposal.md
   694	        design.md
   695	        links.yaml
   696	
   697	repo-A/
   698	  openspec/
   699	    changes/
   700	      add-3ds-api/
   701	        .openspec.yaml
   702	        tasks.md
   703	        specs/
   704	
   705	repo-B/
   706	  openspec/
   707	    changes/
   708	      add-3ds-web/
   709	        .openspec.yaml
   710	        tasks.md
   711	        specs/
   712	```
   713	
   714	The initiative holds the shared planning layer:
   715	
   716	- proposal / intent
   717	- shared design and tradeoffs
   718	- participating teams
   719	- impacted repos
   720	- milestones, risks, and dependencies
   721	- links to repo-local changes
   722	
   723	Each repo-local change holds the execution layer for that repo:
   724	
   725	- repo-specific tasks
   726	- delta specs
   727	- local implementation status
   728	- optional local notes that should archive with that repo's work
   729	
   730	Cross-repo linking still matters, but it should hang off the initiative and the repo-local changes:
   731	
   732	```yaml
   733	# billing-service/openspec/changes/add-3ds/.openspec.yaml
   734	schema: spec-driven
   735	created: 2026-04-12
   736	initiative: add-3ds
   737	links:
   738	  - project: github.com/fission/web-client
   739	    change: add-3ds-checkout
   740	  - project: github.com/fission/ios-client
   741	    change: add-3ds-checkout
   742	```
   743	
   744	Each repo still holds its own change with its own deltas. A cross-repo effort is represented as one initiative plus N linked single-repo changes. This is preferable to a single mega-change because:
   745	- Shared planning has one truthful home
   746	- Each repo's change goes through its own archive cycle
   747	- No need to resolve cross-repo file paths in delta specs
   748	- Teams can move at different speeds (web ships before iOS)
   749	
   750	For small single-repo work, a repo-local change may still be "good enough" as both plan and execution bundle. The initiative-first split matters once work becomes cross-team, cross-module, cross-repo, or otherwise coordination-heavy.
   751	
   752	### Decision: Stable Project Identifiers, Not Paths
   753	
   754	Cross-repo links must use **stable project identifiers**, not filesystem paths.
   755	
   756	- **Canonical form:** A normalized `host/org/repo` tuple (e.g., `github.com/fission/web-client`).
   757	- **Authoring shorthand:** The CLI accepts `org/repo` (e.g., `fission/web-client`) and infers the host from the current repo's remote.
   758	- **Relative paths are never the durable identifier.** They may exist only as cached local resolution results.
   759	
   760	### Decision: Offline-First Resolution
   761	
   762	The CLI resolves project identifiers to local paths using an offline-first chain:
   763	
   764	1. **Explicit paths** passed for the current run (e.g., CLI flags, ad-hoc multi-root).
   765	2. **Local OpenSpec repo registry** — a persistent mapping in `~/.config/openspec/` or `~/.local/share/openspec/` (see `src/core/global-config.ts`).
   766	3. **Parent directory scanning** — scan known parent directories for git checkouts whose remotes match the target identifier.
   767	4. **Unresolved** — if no local path is found, leave the target unresolved and continue with a partial workspace. The CLI must not fail.
   768	
   769	The registry is populated progressively: when the CLI discovers a clone (via scanning or user prompt), it persists the mapping for future resolution. The registry also stores "known scan roots" (e.g., `~/work/`) so scanning improves over time without upfront configuration.
   770	
   771	### Decision: Informational References Only (v1)
   772	
   773	Spec-level cross-repo references are **documentation-only pointers**:
   774	
   775	```yaml
   776	# web-client/openspec/specs/checkout/spec.md frontmatter
   777	references:
   778	  - project: github.com/fission/contracts-service
   779	    spec: checkout-contract
   780	```
   781	
   782	- The CLI does **not** fail validation because a referenced cross-repo spec is missing or unresolved.
   783	- The CLI **does** surface references to humans and agents when planning, viewing, or applying changes.
   784	- Stronger guarantees (e.g., staleness warnings, cross-repo validation) are an opt-in layer added later — via `lint`, `doctor`, or a feature flag — not baseline behavior.
   785	
   786	This avoids accidentally committing OpenSpec to a full dependency graph system before the use cases justify it.
   787	
   788	### Decision: Explicit Owner Repo for Shared Contracts
   789	
   790	When a spec cannot be mapped to a single implementation repo (e.g., a shared API contract):
   791	
   792	- **One repo must be the explicit owner.** This can be a dedicated "contracts" repo, or whichever repo is the natural source of truth.
   793	- **Other repos reference the owning repo's spec** via informational references (see above).
   794	- **There is no default "pure spec repo" pattern.** Separating spec ownership from code ownership too aggressively makes agent execution awkward and diffuses responsibility.
   795	
   796	### Monorepo vs. Multi-Repo Summary
   797	
   798	| Concern | Monorepo | Multi-Repo |
   799	|---------|----------|------------|
   800	| **Spec organization** | Nested specs inside one `openspec/` (Model B) | Each repo has its own `openspec/` |
AI: [{'name': 'read_file', 'args': {'file_path': '/Users/chunming.liu/learn-openspec/OpenSpec/openspec/explorations/workspace-architecture.md', 'limit': 200, 'offset': 800}, 'id': 'call_bbd3521471e644a8946e3de5', 'type': 'tool_call'}]
Tool:    801	| **Cross-cutting specs** | Nested under a `contracts/` or `shared/` directory | Dedicated owner repo, others reference it |
   802	| **Planning object** | Initiative optional for simple work, useful for large cross-team efforts | Initiative is the primary coordination object |
   803	| **Changes** | One or more repo-local changes can implement one initiative | Linked per-repo changes implement one initiative |
   804	| **Relationships** | References (no inheritance in v1) | Project identifier links, informational only |
   805	| **Workspace** | Usually not needed, but can host initiative planning for complex work | Coordination workspace hosts initiative planning; optional manifest for reuse |
   806	
   807	### Implementation Path
   808	
   809	1. **Define initiative artifacts** — add an initiative format for shared planning in coordination workspaces.
   810	2. **Extend change metadata** — let repo-local changes point at an initiative and linked sibling changes.
   811	3. **Extend spec metadata** — add `references` field for cross-repo spec pointers.
   812	4. **Build project resolution** — implement the offline-first resolution chain and local registry.
   813	5. **Build initiative and link views** — commands that resolve and display the initiative graph plus linked repo-local changes.
   814	6. **Support ad-hoc multi-root** — "add these dirs for this run" or "derive roots from this initiative's links."
   815	7. **Optional workspace manifest** — add saved workspaces only if teams demonstrate reuse patterns.
   816	
   817	Nested specs (Model B inside a single repo) are a prerequisite for clean monorepo support and should be tackled first, as outlined in #662.
   818	
   819	---
   820	
   821	## Summary
   822	
   823	| Question | Status | Notes |
   824	|----------|--------|-------|
   825	| Profile UX | Decided | `openspec config profile` with presets |
   826	| Config layering | Decided | Two layers: global + project (no workspace layer) |
   827	| Spec organization | **Direction set** | Nested specs per repo, explicit owner repos for shared contracts, references for cross-repo context |
   828	| Spec philosophy | Direction set | Behavior-first contracts, progressive rigor, and agent-aligned authoring |
   829	| Spec inheritance | **Decided** | References only, no inheritance in v1 |
   830	| Initiative / planning model | **Direction set** | Initiative-first planning for larger work, with repo-local changes as execution artifacts |
   831	| Multi-repo support | **Direction set** | Linked per-repo changes under shared initiatives; workspace is coordination, not canonical execution storage |
   832	| Dependency tracking | **Decided** | Out of scope for v1; references are informational only |
   833	| Cross-repo resolution | **Decided** | Offline-first resolution chain with local registry |
   834	| Shared contracts | **Decided** | Explicit owner repo required; no default pure-spec-repo pattern |
   835	
   836	### Key Insight
   837	
   838	The "workspace" question is really two separate questions:
   839	1. **Config/profile scope** → Solved with global + project (no workspace needed)
   840	2. **Plan vs. execution organization** → Direction set: initiatives coordinate, repo-local changes implement, workspace remains a coordination layer
   841	
   842	These should be separate changes with separate explorations.
   843	
   844	---

Tool:      1	# Context Store And Initiatives Direction
     2	
     3	This document captures the suggested direction from the workspace/initiative
     4	discussion. The main shift is that "workspace" should not be the durable shared
     5	planning object. The durable shared object is a synced context store, and
     6	initiatives are one opinionated collection inside it.
     7	
     8	## Core Model
     9	
    10	```text
    11	Context Store
    12	  = synced shared content container
    13	
    14	Collection
    15	  = mounted content system inside a store
    16	
    17	Initiatives
    18	  = first major collection for cross-team implementation context
    19	
    20	Workspace
    21	  = local working view over context stores and repos
    22	
    23	Change
    24	  = repo/team-owned implementation plan
    25	```
    26	
    27	The clean rule:
    28	
    29	```text
    30	Context stores sync truth.
    31	Collections shape truth.
    32	Initiatives coordinate work.
    33	Workspaces open local views.
    34	Changes implement repo-owned slices.
    35	```
    36	
    37	## Locked Product Boundary
    38	
    39	The workspace-to-initiative pivot is now the product boundary for future
    40	coordination work:
    41	
    42	- A workspace is a regenerable, machine-local working view. It maps context
    43	  stores, initiatives, projects, repos, and folders to paths the current user can
    44	  open.
    45	- A context store is the durable synced container for shared files.
    46	- An initiative is the durable coordination object for cross-team or cross-repo
    47	  implementation context.
    48	- A repo-local change remains the implementation plan owned by the repo or team
    49	  doing the work.
    50	
    51	This supersedes the older model where a workspace-level `changes/` tree owned
    52	the canonical shared plan for cross-repo work. Existing workspace-planning
    53	behavior can remain as beta or legacy infrastructure, but it should not steer
    54	new lifecycle design.
    55	
    56	Workspace roadmap disposition:
    57	
    58	- Keep setup, link, relink, list, open, update, and doctor.
    59	- Keep linked repos and folders visible for exploration before a change exists.
    60	- Keep workspace-local agent guidance as local view setup, refreshed by
    61	  `workspace update`.
    62	- Defer workspace apply, verify, and archive until initiatives can link to
    63	  repo-owned OpenSpec changes.
    64	- Defer branch/worktree orchestration, multi-repo apply, strong cross-repo
    65	  validation, and dependency graph enforcement.
    66	
    67	## Agent-First UX
    68	
    69	The primary user experience for initiatives is expected to be agent-driven:
    70	
    71	```text
    72	Using initiative billing-launch, explore the API work and create a proposal.
    73	```
    74	
    75	The user should not need to know every command. OpenSpec should expose small,
    76	structured CLI primitives that an agent can use to:
    77	
    78	- find the intended initiative across registered context stores
    79	- read canonical initiative files from the context store
    80	- create or link a repo-local OpenSpec change
    81	- use workspace state for local repo and folder views
    82	- respect edit boundaries instead of treating every opened folder as editable
    83	
    84	The CLI is therefore the agent's tool surface, not the whole user workflow.
    85	Prefer explicit, machine-readable commands such as `initiative show --json`,
    86	`new change --initiative ...`, and workspace local-view commands over broad
    87	interactive flows as the first slice.
    88	
    89	Canonical initiative context should stay in the context store. Repo-local
    90	changes should reference the initiative rather than checking in copied snapshots
    91	of initiative prose. If an agent needs a compact context pack, OpenSpec can
    92	generate that as command output from the live initiative context.
    93	
    94	## Context Store
    95	
    96	A context store is the shared/synced folder of files. It is content-agnostic.
    97	It should not know what an initiative is.
    98	
    99	Example:
   100

Tool:      1	# Concepts
     2	
     3	This guide explains the core ideas behind OpenSpec and how they fit together. For practical usage, see [Getting Started](getting-started.md) and [Workflows](workflows.md).
     4	
     5	## Philosophy
     6	
     7	OpenSpec is built around four principles:
     8	
     9	```
    10	fluid not rigid         — no phase gates, work on what makes sense
    11	iterative not waterfall — learn as you build, refine as you go
    12	easy not complex        — lightweight setup, minimal ceremony
    13	brownfield-first        — works with existing codebases, not just greenfield
    14	```
    15	
    16	### Why These Principles Matter
    17	
    18	**Fluid not rigid.** Traditional spec systems lock you into phases: first you plan, then you implement, then you're done. OpenSpec is more flexible — you can create artifacts in any order that makes sense for your work.
    19	
    20	**Iterative not waterfall.** Requirements change. Understanding deepens. What seemed like a good approach at the start might not hold up after you see the codebase. OpenSpec embraces this reality.
    21	
    22	**Easy not complex.** Some spec frameworks require extensive setup, rigid formats, or heavyweight processes. OpenSpec stays out of your way. Initialize in seconds, start working immediately, customize only if you need to.
    23	
    24	**Brownfield-first.** Most software work isn't building from scratch — it's modifying existing systems. OpenSpec's delta-based approach makes it easy to specify changes to existing behavior, not just describe new systems.
    25	
    26	## The Big Picture
    27	
    28	OpenSpec organizes your work into two main areas:
    29	
    30	```
    31	┌────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
    32	│                        openspec/                                   │
    33	│                                                                    │
    34	│   ┌─────────────────────┐      ┌───────────────────────────────┐   │
    35	│   │       specs/        │      │         changes/              │   │
    36	│   │                     │      │                               │   │
    37	│   │  Source of truth    │◄─────│  Proposed modifications       │   │
    38	│   │  How your system    │ merge│  Each change = one folder     │   │
    39	│   │  currently works    │      │  Contains artifacts + deltas  │   │
    40	│   │                     │      │                               │   │
    41	│   └─────────────────────┘      └───────────────────────────────┘   │
    42	│                                                                    │
    43	└────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
    44	```
    45	
    46	**Specs** are the source of truth — they describe how your system currently behaves.
    47	
    48	**Changes** are proposed modifications — they live in separate folders until you're ready to merge them.
    49	
    50	This separation is key. You can work on multiple changes in parallel without conflicts. You can review a change before it affects the main specs. And when you archive a change, its deltas merge cleanly into the source of truth.
    51	
    52	## Coordination Workspaces
    53	
    54	Workspace support is in beta. The local-view model below is the current direction, but external automation, integrations, and long-lived workflows should still treat command behavior, state files, and JSON output as evolving.
    55	
    56	The commands below provide the first setup flow for opening local views over linked repos or folders.
    57	
    58	Repo-local OpenSpec projects are the right default when one repo owns the planning, implementation, and archive flow. Some work spans several repos or folders. For that case, an OpenSpec coordination workspace is a machine-local view that keeps linked paths, opener state, and agent setup together.
    59	
    60	The workspace mental model is:
    61	
    62	```text
    63	workspace     = private local view over context stores, initiatives, repos, and folders
    64	context store = durable shared context container
    65	initiative    = durable coordination context inside a context store
    66	link          = a stable name for a repo or folder the workspace can resolve locally
    67	change        = one planned piece of work; implementation belongs in the owning repo
    68	```
    69	
    70	A workspace has a different shape from a repo-local project:
    71	
    72	```text
    73	getGlobalDataDir()/workspaces/<workspace-name>/
    74	├── .openspec-workspace/
    75	│   └── view.yaml                  # Private local view record
    76	├── AGENTS.md                      # Generated runtime guidance
    77	└── <workspace-name>.code-workspace # Generated editor workspace file
    78	```
    79	
    80	Repo-local OpenSpec state keeps the existing shape:
    81	
    82	```text
    83	repo-root/
    84	└── openspec/
    85	    ├── specs/
    86	    └── changes/
    87	```
    88	
    89	Root-level `workspace.yaml` files are not OpenSpec workspace state. Workspace state is namespaced under `.openspec-workspace/`, so other tools can keep owning root-level files with the same name.
    90	
    91	That distinction matters. The workspace folder is a local coordination surface for opening and inspecting linked repos or folders. Each repo's `openspec/` directory remains the home for repo-owned specs, repo-local changes, and implementation planning. Users do not need to run repo-local `openspec init` inside a workspace folder.
    92	
    93	Stable link names are how a workspace refers to repos and folders. The private workspace record keeps names such as `api`, `web`, or `checkout` and maps them to this runtime's local paths.
    94	
    95	```yaml
    96	# .openspec-workspace/view.yaml
    97	version: 1
    98	name: platform
    99	context: null
   100	links:

Tool:      1	---
     2	name: beautiful-feishu-whiteboard
     3	version: 1.1.0
     4	description: >
     5	  A library of 35 curated colour palette styles for building beautiful, editable Feishu / Lark
     6	  (飞书) whiteboards from SVG. Use this whenever the user wants to create or draw a Feishu whiteboard,
     7	  infographic, diagram, poster, or visual explainer and wants it to look polished, on brand, or in a
     8	  particular aesthetic, or when they name a style. The agent figures out what board the user wants,
     9	  asks about the visual vibe, picks a fitting style from the catalogue, generates an editable Feishu
    10	  whiteboard, returns the doc link and the rendered image, and offers to switch styles. Requires
    11	  lark-cli (npm @larksuite/cli) installed and authenticated, and a Feishu/Lark account.
    12	---
    13	
    14	# Beautiful Feishu Whiteboard
    15	
    16	A design system skill: **35 colour palette styles** plus the verified **hard rules** of the Feishu
    17	SVG whiteboard medium, so you can build gorgeous, on brand, *editable* whiteboards.
    18	
    19	This is **not** an auto layout chart generator. **You** compose the layout. A template gives you a
    20	**palette and mood**; [`RULES.md`](RULES.md) gives you the medium's hard limits. The board you
    21	produce is a real, editable Feishu whiteboard inside a doc, not a screenshot.
    22	
    23	## When to use
    24	- The user wants a Feishu / Lark whiteboard, infographic, diagram, poster, or visual explainer that
    25	  should look good, branded, or in a specific aesthetic.
    26	- The user names a style, or points at one of the templates.
    27	- The user gives content ("explain X as a whiteboard", "turn this into a board") and wants it
    28	  visual and editable inside Feishu.
    29	
    30	## Step 0: prerequisites (check before doing anything)
    31	Run [`scripts/preflight.sh`](scripts/preflight.sh), or check manually:
    32	- **Node 20 or newer.**
    33	- **`lark-cli`** (npm package **`@larksuite/cli`**), installed **and authenticated**. If missing:
    34	  `npm install -g @larksuite/cli`, then `lark-cli config init` (scan the QR), then `lark-cli auth login`.
    35	- **`@larksuite/whiteboard-cli`**, used via `npx`, auto downloads, no install needed.
    36	- A **Feishu / Lark account**. The board is written to the user's own tenant.
    37	
    38	If `lark-cli` is missing or not authenticated, tell the user exactly how to install and
    39	authenticate, then stop. You cannot write a board without it.
    40	
    41	## How to run the conversation
    42	
    43	1. **Understand the board.** Find out what the user wants on it: the content, the purpose, the
    44	   audience. If it is unclear, ask one short question before building.
    45	2. **Ask about the vibe.** Ask the user what visual style they want, for example playful or
    46	   professional, any colour or mood preference, or a brand to match. Offer to pick for them if they
    47	   have no preference. If they already named a style, skip ahead.
    48	3. **Pick a style.** Use [`CATALOG.md`](CATALOG.md) to match the content type and the requested vibe
    49	   and formality to one of the 35 styles. Tell the user which style you chose and why, in one line.
    50	4. **Build it.** Read [`RULES.md`](RULES.md) and the chosen [`templates/<slug>/design.md`](templates/),
    51	   then:
    52	   - Compose the SVG in a logical space about 1600 to 1700 wide, in that palette, with **native
    53	     shapes only** (rect, rounded rect, circle, ellipse, connectors, text). Lay the content out
    54	     however reads best. Every label is a `<text>`; never set `font-family`.
    55	   - **Only the content goes on the board, never the instruction behind it.** Do not print the
    56	     user's prompt, scope notes, source citations, the chosen style name, or any "summary of… /
    57	     来源… / 风格…" meta line onto the canvas — that looks like a homework header. Such context
    58	     belongs in your chat reply. (See the "Never echo the user's instructions" rule in `RULES.md`.)
    59	   - **Render it, then look at the image and correct yourself**: fix text overflow, tight margins or
    60	     padding, numerals touching edges, accidental overlaps, and clipping. Iterate render, view, fix
    61	     until it is clean. (Commands and the full checklist are in `RULES.md`.)
    62	   - Write it into a Feishu doc as an editable whiteboard, then view the live board image too and fix
    63	     any remaining layout issues.
    64	5. **Deliver.** Give the user **both**: the **Feishu doc link** and the **rendered image** itself, so
    65	   they can see it without opening the doc. Then tell them they can **switch to a different style**
    66	   any time, and you will re render the same content in the new palette.
    67	
    68	## Files
    69	- **[`RULES.md`](RULES.md)**: the hard rules and the exact build, write, and verify commands. Always read this.
    70	- **[`CATALOG.md`](CATALOG.md)**: every style with its vibe, formality, and what it is good for. Use it to choose.
    71	- **[`templates/<slug>/design.md`](templates/)**: one per style, the colour palette and how to use it.
    72	- **[`scripts/preflight.sh`](scripts/preflight.sh)**: dependency and auth check.

Tool:      1	# Feishu SVG Whiteboard — Medium Rules
     2	
     3	These apply to **every** board, regardless of template. A template gives you a **color palette
     4	and a mood**; this file is the **hard limits of the medium**. All of it is verified empirically on
     5	the real board (the image export and the GUI behave differently — see notes below).
     6	
     7	## Hard rules
     8	
     9	- **One font.** The board hardcodes a single font (Noto Sans SC). Never set `font-family`. Type is
    10	  size / weight / casing / letter-spacing only — there is no typeface choice.
    11	- **Text lives in `<text>` / `<tspan>`** — never outline glyphs as `<path>`.
    12	- **Shape vocabulary is native-only — a rectangles-and-circles tool.** Build everything from
    13	  `<rect>` (sharp or rounded `rx`), `<circle>`, `<ellipse>`, straight `<line>` / `<polyline>`
    14	  connectors, and `<text>` — these become real editable shapes. `<polygon>` and any curved/bezier
    15	  `<path>` embed as flat **images** (not shapes, render lumpy) — at most one simple triangle/diamond
    16	  as a minor accent, never structural. **No freeform / organic / illustrative shapes** (blobs,
    17	  leaves, petals, waves, coral, fans, flowers, stars, confetti, doodles, mascots, "hand-cut"
    18	  silhouettes). If a reference is organic, keep only its **palette** and rebuild with rects + circles.
    19	- **Arrows = native connectors, never hand-drawn heads.** To put an arrowhead on a line, give the
    20	  `<line>` or `<polyline>` a **`marker-end`** (and `marker-start` for a double-headed arrow)
    21	  pointing at a `<marker>` in `<defs>`. The board converts these into **native connectors with a
    22	  clean built-in arrowhead**, and the arrow takes the line's `stroke` colour. **Never draw an
    23	  arrowhead as a separate `<polygon>` / triangle** — a polygon embeds as a flat image and renders
    24	  rough, squiggly, and hand-drawn (unprofessional). One marker definition serves the whole board:
    25	  ```svg
    26	  <defs>
    27	    <marker id="arrow" markerWidth="12" markerHeight="12" refX="9" refY="4"
    28	            orient="auto" markerUnits="strokeWidth"><path d="M0 0 L10 4 L0 8 z"/></marker>
    29	  </defs>
    30	  <line x1="100" y1="80" x2="360" y2="80" stroke="#0D4FA8" stroke-width="3" marker-end="url(#arrow)"/>
    31	  <!-- right-angled connector: polyline (H/V points) + marker-end -->
    32	  <polyline points="100,140 240,140 240,220" fill="none" stroke="#0D4FA8" stroke-width="3" marker-end="url(#arrow)"/>
    33	  <!-- double-headed (e.g. read/write): add marker-start too -->
    34	  <line x1="100" y1="280" x2="360" y2="280" stroke="#0D4FA8" stroke-width="3" marker-start="url(#arrow)" marker-end="url(#arrow)"/>
    35	  ```
    36	  (The marker's own shape/colour is ignored on the board; it just signals "put a native arrowhead
    37	  here." Keep the line straight or right-angled — those map to clean `straight` / `right_angled` connectors.)
    38	  **The classic defect** is a `<line>` plus a separate little `<polyline>` / `<polygon>` chevron drawn
    39	  at its tip — that chevron IS a hand-drawn arrowhead. Delete it and put `marker-end` on the line.
    40	  **Mandatory self-check before you write the board:** run
    41	  `grep -nE '<polygon|<polyline' <dir>/diagram.svg` — every `<polygon>`, and every short `<polyline>`
    42	  shaped like a triangle/chevron at a line's endpoint, is a defect to convert. (A `<polyline>` is fine
    43	  ONLY as a right-angled connector path that itself carries `marker-end` and has no separate chevron.)
    44	  **This matters most when you START FROM or EDIT an existing SVG** (a gallery template, a previous
    45	  board, a translation): its arrows may predate this rule and still be hand-drawn — re-check and
    46	  convert them, don't just edit the text around them.
    47	- **Forbidden** (break or flatten to a static image): any gradient, `<filter>`, `<pattern>`,
    48	  `<clipPath>`, `<mask>`, blur.
    49	- **Opacity is ignored.** `opacity` / `fill-opacity` / `stroke-opacity` all render fully opaque.
    50	  To get a paler tint, use a **solid lighter hex** — never alpha. (To fake a translucent overlap,
    51	  paint the overlap region as its own solid darker shape.)
    52	- **Text color is unrestricted on the live board** (any hex; the GUI's ~9 presets are not a limit) —
    53	  **but the image export renders text color unreliably (often black).** Judge text color via
    54	  `+query --output_as raw` (stored hex) or the live board, **never** the exported PNG. Light/cream
    55	  text works on saturated-dark fills when it's large and bold; small light text on near-black is unreliable.
    56	- **Shadows.** No blur/filter shadows (unsupported). Hard **offset shadows are allowed and fine** —
    57	  draw one as a **solid duplicate of the SAME shape** offset behind the real one (never a blur). It must
    58	  match the element's shape exactly: same `rx` (a pill's shadow is a pill, not a rect), same `width`/`height`,
    59	  and inside the same `rotate()` group so it tilts with the element. Default can still be flat — depth also
    60	  comes from flat color blocks, role-swaps, and scale.
    61	- **No decorative micro-chrome.** No ornamental kicker/footer/slug/metadata labels. Every text
    62	  element must be load-bearing and legible (≥ ~16px, strong contrast); keep small text inside
    63	  high-contrast panels, never small on a colored canvas.
    64	- **Never echo the user's instructions or your own process onto the board.** The board shows the
    65	  **content** — never the request that produced it, the inputs you read, or how you built it. A
    66	  whiteboard is a finished artifact, not a homework submission, so it carries no "here's what you
    67	  asked for" framing. Cut every meta / process line, for example:
    68	  - scope or task notes — *"整理范围：仅总结张睿发言；问答部分按她的回答归纳"*, *"本图涵盖第 3-5 章"*
    69	  - source citations — *"来源：会议逐字稿 00:06:28–00:52:58 的张睿发言"*, *"based on the attached doc"*
    70	  - the chosen style / template name — *"风格：Specimen Bold"*, *"Riso Brut template"*
    71	  - audience / format directions, restatements of the prompt, and *"summary of… / 总结自…"* framing
    72	  - dates, tokens, file paths, or tooling you were not explicitly asked to display
    73	
    74	  A **title may name the subject** (e.g. *"张睿 · 发言总结"* is fine — that is the topic), but nothing
    75	  on the board may describe the task, the source material, or the tool. **Litmus test:** if a line is
    76	  addressed to the person who asked (or to a grader) rather than being a real part of the artifact,
    77	  delete it. Put that kind of context in your chat reply to the user, never on the canvas.
    78	- **Transforms:** `translate` / `rotate` / `scale` are safe; avoid `skewX` / `skewY` / `matrix(...)`.
    79	- **No fixed canvas.** No 16:9, no scaler. Work in a logical coordinate space (≈1600–1700 wide) and
    80	  let content define the bounds.
    81	- **Text reflows by character** (CJK ≈ 1em, Latin ≈ 0.6em). Pad boxes generously; never fit text to
    82	  the pixel; wrap long lines across `<tspan>`s rather than shrinking.
    83	
    84	## Workflow
    85	
    86	1. Pick a **template** (`templates/<slug>/design.md`) for the palette + mood, and read it.
    87	2. Pick the narrative shape (pipeline / stages / comparison / system map / timeline) and write the
    88	   SVG in a logical coord space (≈1600–1700 wide); **native shapes only**; every label a `<text>`.
    89	3. **Render and LOOK at it — then fix what you see.** This is the most important step:
    90	   - `npx -y @larksuite/whiteboard-cli@^0.2.11 -i <dir>/diagram.svg -o <dir>/diagram.png -f svg`
    91	   - `npx -y @larksuite/whiteboard-cli@^0.2.11 -i <dir>/diagram.svg -f svg --check`
    92	   - **Open `diagram.png` and actually view it.** Correct the common problems before moving on, and
    93	     iterate render → look → fix until it's clean:
    94	     - **text overflow** — text spilling out of its box or past the canvas edge,
    95	     - **margins / padding** — content flush to the canvas edge, or a numeral/title touching a box's
    96	       top/side with no breathing room,
    97	     - **overlaps** — shapes or labels colliding unintentionally (a duplicate-offset shadow or an
    98	       intentional overlap is fine; an accidental one is not),
    99	     - **clipping** — anything cut off on the right/bottom.
   100	     - **hand-drawn arrowheads** — run `grep -nE '<polygon|<polyline' <dir>/diagram.svg` and convert any
   101	       chevron/triangle arrowhead to a `marker-end` (see the Arrows rule above). Do this every time,
   102	       and especially when you started from an existing SVG whose arrows may predate the rule.
   103	   - (`--check` flags `text-overflow`/`node-overlap`; intentional overlaps, off-canvas bleed, or a
   104	     centered long Latin headline may report as warnings — judge with your eyes, not just the linter.)
   105	4. **Write it into Feishu as an editable whiteboard**, then **look at the real board too:**
   106	   `npx -y @larksuite/whiteboard-cli@^0.2.11 -i <dir>/diagram.svg --to openapi --format json | lark-cli whiteboard +update --whiteboard-token <tok> --source - --input_format raw --idempotent-token <unique> --overwrite --as user`
   107	   then `lark-cli whiteboard +query --whiteboard-token <tok> --output_as image --output <dir> --as user`,
   108	   view it, and fix any remaining layout issues. The export is faithful for **layout, shapes, fills,
   109	   opacity** — but **not text color** (verify color via `--output_as raw` or the live doc, not the PNG).
   110	
   111	### Creating the doc + whiteboard block to write into
   112	
   113	If the user didn't give a target whiteboard, create one:
   114	```bash
   115	# new doc with an empty whiteboard block; grab the block_token from the response
   116	lark-cli docs +create --api-version v2 \
   117	  --content '<title>My board</title><whiteboard type="blank"></whiteboard>' --as user
   118	```
   119	Then write the SVG to that `block_token` with the `whiteboard +update` command in step 4. To add a
   120	board to an existing doc the user gave, fetch it (`lark-cli docs +fetch`) or append a
   121	`<whiteboard type="blank"></whiteboard>` block, and use that block's token.

Tool:      1	# Style Catalogue
     2	
     3	Use this to pick a style for the user, based on the **level** (how restrained or bold it feels),
     4	the **formality**, and the **vibe** they want. Any style can carry almost any content, so choose by
     5	feel. If the user named a style or a clear preference, honour it. Otherwise infer from the tone they
     6	want and propose one. After generating, always tell the user they can switch to any other style.
     7	
     8	The 35 styles are grouped into three levels, from restrained to bold:
     9	
    10	- **Restrained** — monochrome, two-tone, calm and muted; not colourful. For serious, quiet, editorial boards.
    11	- **Balanced** — a confident accent or two on a clean ground; the versatile middle. Most general work lives here.
    12	- **Bold** — loud, maximalist, lots of bright colour. For playful, high-energy, attention-grabbing boards.
    13	
    14	**Formality key:** **Low** = playful / casual, **Medium** = confident / modern, **High** =
    15	restrained / refined / institutional.
    16	
    17	## Restrained
    18	
    19	| Style | Formality | Vibe |

    66	
    67	## How to choose
    68	
    69	1. **Level first.** How loud should the board feel? Quiet / serious leans **Restrained**; playful or
    70	   high-energy leans **Bold**; most general work sits comfortably in **Balanced**.
    71	2. **Formality second.** Within the level, match how formal it should read (Low / Medium / High).
    72	3. **Vibe third.** Narrow by the user's mood or colour preference (the Vibe column).
    73	4. **When unsure, default to a safe or balanced style.**
    74	5. Always offer to switch afterwards. Swapping style means re rendering the same content in a
    75	   different template's palette.

    35	---
    36	
    37	# Editorial Forest — Feishu SVG Whiteboard Design System
    38	
    39	A deep forest green, a dusty rose, and an oat-cream page: three surfaces and a couple of near-duplicate
    40	shades, used with editorial discipline. Pick one surface to anchor a scene, keep the color count low,
    41	and let the green-and-pink pairing carry the identity.
    42	
    43	## Color
    44	
    45	- **Green** (`#2e4a2a`): The deep forest primary. A slide surface, a tile fill, a card border, a divider color, and the primary heading color on cream. The most distinctive surface in the system.
    46	- **Green Deep** (`#243a21`): A darker green used mainly as text color on pink surfaces, where pure green would lack contrast.
    47	- **Green Lite** (`#3a5a36`): A lighter green used as a fill when two greens need to sit beside each other. Carries pink text.
    48	- **Pink** (`#e89cb1`): A dusty rose. A surface, a tile fill, and the on-green text color. The system's primary accent.
    49	- **Pink Deep** (`#d27e96`): A slightly darker rose used only as a border on pink-filled tiles.
    50	- **Cream** (`#efe7d4`): The oat paper surface. The default background on non-cover scenes, and the on-green body text color.
    51	- **Cream 2** (`#e6dcc4`): A subtly darker cream tile on the cream ground. Pair with a 2px green border so it reads as distinct.
    52	- **Ink** (`#1a1a17`): Body text on cream surfaces. A warm near-black, never pure black.
    53	
    54	## Rules
    55	
    56	This template is the **palette + mood** only. Every medium constraint (native shapes, opacity ignored, the text-color export caveat, no gradients/filters/shadows, reflow) and the build/verify workflow live in **[`../../RULES.md`](../../RULES.md)** — read it before building.
